        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206441173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2520914200261518377">How wonderfully freaked out is this book? It's about a young, introverted zen priest who becomes obssessed with a six hundred year old temple to the exclusion of everything else in his life, and then decides it has to be burned down to the ground. And it actually happened! Mishima is just brilliant at sucking you into the world of Mizoguchi's damaged neurosis. And almost every paragraph has at least one mind-fuck brilliant observation about beauty, ugliness, love, obsession, destruction, what ha</span>
  <span id="freeText2520914200261518377" style="display:none">How wonderfully freaked out is this book? It's about a young, introverted zen priest who becomes obssessed with a six hundred year old temple to the exclusion of everything else in his life, and then decides it has to be burned down to the ground. And it actually happened! Mishima is just brilliant at sucking you into the world of Mizoguchi's damaged neurosis. And almost every paragraph has at least one mind-fuck brilliant observation about beauty, ugliness, love, obsession, destruction, what have you. It's not exactly a novel, and not exactly a treatise on aesthetics, but some delerious hybrid of both. I feel like this book is an example of the exact right author finding the exact right story.</span>

<div id="review_600413537" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/600413537" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/600413537?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679752707" href="/book/show/763091.The_Temple_of_the_Golden_Pavilion">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer600413537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7899354534593792159">“To be sure, there are times when the reality of the outer world seems to be waiting for me, folding its arms as it were, while I was struggling to free myself. But the reality that is waiting for me is not a fresh reality. When finally I reach the outer world after all my efforts, all that I find is a reality that has instantly changed colour and gone out of focus- a reality that has lost the freshness that I had considered fitting for myself, and that gives off a half-putrid odour."<br/><br/>Mishima is </span>
  <span id="freeText7899354534593792159" style="display:none">“To be sure, there are times when the reality of the outer world seems to be waiting for me, folding its arms as it were, while I was struggling to free myself. But the reality that is waiting for me is not a fresh reality. When finally I reach the outer world after all my efforts, all that I find is a reality that has instantly changed colour and gone out of focus- a reality that has lost the freshness that I had considered fitting for myself, and that gives off a half-putrid odour."<br /><br />Mishima is such a great writer.His protagonist in this novel, Mizoguchi, is truly a sociopath; his creepy obsession with beauty, the Golden Pavilion in particular, threatens to consume him. He is very philosophical to the point of arrogance. All in all, a very well-written book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7899354534593792159" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_183430807" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/183430807" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5555347-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442474198p2/5555347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/183430807?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/5555347-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780399504884" href="/book/show/1089517.The_Temple_of_the_Golden_Pavilion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555347-john?shelf=favorite-novels">favorite-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183430807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12159099889372817773">	Yukio Mishima's <i>The Temple of the Golden Pavilion</i> is a meditation on the relationship between words and action, beauty and ugliness, and Being and nothingness. In this book, which is one of Mishima's best novels, these themes are treated with considerable patience and depth, giving readers great insight into the philosophical issues that preoccupied Mishima for the entirety of his writing career; all the way up to his own ritual suicide by seppuku in 1970.<br/>	The plot of the story concerns a Japan</span>
  <span id="freeText12159099889372817773" style="display:none">	Yukio Mishima's <i>The Temple of the Golden Pavilion</i> is a meditation on the relationship between words and action, beauty and ugliness, and Being and nothingness. In this book, which is one of Mishima's best novels, these themes are treated with considerable patience and depth, giving readers great insight into the philosophical issues that preoccupied Mishima for the entirety of his writing career; all the way up to his own ritual suicide by seppuku in 1970.<br />	The plot of the story concerns a Japanese college student, named Mizoguchi, who serves as an acolyte at a Zen Buddhist temple in Kyoto during and shortly after World War II. Mizoguchi is a stutterer; a disability that is symbolic of his separation from the world around him. He is preoccupied with the incongruity that exists between his own inner, subjective experience, and the world that exists beyond his subjectivity. He is alienated from everything and everyone outside of himself, feeling alone and misunderstood until he becomes friends with Kashiwagi, a club-footed student who attends the same college. Mizoguchi's friendship with Kashiwagi pushes Mizoguchi to confront his own alienation and to finally overcome it through an act of destruction: the torching of the Temple of the Golden Pavilion.<br />	The Temple of the Golden Pavilion represents the impossibility of all abstract notions of idealized perfection. When Misoguchi was a child, his father described the Golden Temple to him as the most beautiful thing on earth. This description, which resides only in the mind, set a criterion against which the actual, concrete temple was doomed to fall short once Misoguchi was finally to see it in actuality. His disappointment with the appearance of the actual temple provoked a desire to explore further, to look harder, and to expend more effort in order to discover what he had missed. Misoguchi is not satisfied with only the ideal of perfection; he wants to see that idea reflected in the world of objective reality. His frustration with this quest comes to an end only when he discovers the logic of destruction and nothingness.<br />	The beauty of the Golden Temple rests in its lifelessness. Living things, like humans, cannot be truly beautiful since they lack the quality of being eternal and "rigid." Beauty is infinite, and so nothing finite can be absolutely beautiful. The Temple, then, is only beautiful to the degree that it exemplifies a timeless ideal. Yet, the materials out of which the Temple was constructed are subject to decay. These physical materials -- the timbers and the gold foil, the nails and the planks -- provide a medium through which the eternal may speak, but they also hold back the infinite by binding it to something that is itself finite. This was the source of Misoguchi's disappointment upon first seeing the Golden Temple, and he comes to realize that it is only through destruction of the physical manifestation of the Temple that it's essence can be liberated and finally achieve immortality.<br />	The theme of nihilism permeates the entirety of this novel, as it does all of Mishima's work. In one of his other, non-fiction books, titled <i>Sun and Steel</i>, Mishima explicitly applauds the kind of nihilism that Nietzsche called "active nihilism," which strikes out at the void in order to create, destroy and act. The character Misoguchi is an exemplar of just this sort of nihilistic impulse. His only other option would be passive acquiescence to reality as it exists in a fractured condition stretched between the real and the ideal. In the destruction of the physical Temple, he symbolically eradicates his slavery to the real, and thus frees himself to dive into the flow of life, regardless of its imperfections. <br />	My personal feeling is that while Misoguchi's act of rebellion in <i>The Temple of the Golden Pavilion</i> may be emotionally cathartic, metaphysically it changes nothing. Ideals are inescapable for human existence, and though they may cause us distress insofar as we inevitably fall short of their absolute standards, they also spur us on toward the goals and projects that make life worth living. Nihilism, in my view, is not something to be overcome, but something that is part of the human condition. To overcome nihilism is either to die or to become something other than a human.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12159099889372817773" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14146237" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14146237" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/858949-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208350750p2/858949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14146237?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/858949-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/162375.The_Temple_Of_The_Golden_Pavilion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=japan">japan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14146237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13855510324172458511">Mishima is one of the most famous modern Japanese writers and, near as I can tell, a complete nutjob. Or was, anyway. He killed himself by seppuku back in 1970.<br/><br/><i>Kinkakuji</i> is one of his most famous works, and I chose it as a first entry into Mishima because I love reading books set in Kyoto and, well, I've been to Kinkakuji a few times.<br/><br/>My reaction upon seeing it was a lot like the main character's - disappointment. In the book, a young Mizuguchi is told by his father that the Golden Temple is the </span>
  <span id="freeText13855510324172458511" style="display:none">Mishima is one of the most famous modern Japanese writers and, near as I can tell, a complete nutjob. Or was, anyway. He killed himself by seppuku back in 1970.<br /><br /><i>Kinkakuji</i> is one of his most famous works, and I chose it as a first entry into Mishima because I love reading books set in Kyoto and, well, I've been to Kinkakuji a few times.<br /><br />My reaction upon seeing it was a lot like the main character's - disappointment. In the book, a young Mizuguchi is told by his father that the Golden Temple is the most beautiful thing in the world, and so the child - who secretly despises beautiful things - has an idealized vision of it in his mind. And, as we all know, the idealized vision never quite holds up to the reality.<br /><br />The real Kinkakuji is nice, yes, but not quite as nice as you thought it would be. Or at least, not as nice as I thought it would be. I rather prefer the Silver Pavillion - not quite as ostentatious.<br /><br />Anyway, young Mizuguchi, a recluse, a stutterer, becomes as acolyte at Kinkakuji when his father dies. And thus begins a bizarre love-hate obsession with the temple that ends in flames....<br /><br />There's a lot of philosophy in this book. Discussion of What Beauty Is and what we should do with it. Long talks about the transitory nature of human life and the eternity of structure and.... I'm not sure. I was reading a lot of this on the plane, and trying not to kill some small children with the power of my mind.<br /><br />Mishima is one of those authors I want to know more about, though. He's thoughtful and creative, a very good example of post-war Japanese angst, and definitely had a lot of big ideas that he wanted to get out. He gathered a kind of cult following and shocked the nation with his suicide. This is a book that'll take a few re-reads, I think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13855510324172458511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2694151131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2694151131" />
    <a title="Tsung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung"><img alt="Tsung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298808605p2/4992851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2694151131?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tsung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tsung" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung">Tsung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2694151131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6621174182077392880">In 1950, the 600 year old Kinkakuji was burned down by a novice monk. Mishima creates a fictional account of the person and events leading up to its destruction. The success of this novel depends on how successful Mishima is in convincing the reader of the young acolyte’s personality, thoughts and motivations.<br/><br/>The Golden Pavilion in Kyoto is beautiful, but not captivating. It may be that it is a reconstruction or it may be the thousands of tourists that rob it of its Zenness. Mizoguchi had high e</span>
  <span id="freeText6621174182077392880" style="display:none">In 1950, the 600 year old Kinkakuji was burned down by a novice monk. Mishima creates a fictional account of the person and events leading up to its destruction. The success of this novel depends on how successful Mishima is in convincing the reader of the young acolyte’s personality, thoughts and motivations.<br /><br />The Golden Pavilion in Kyoto is beautiful, but not captivating. It may be that it is a reconstruction or it may be the thousands of tourists that rob it of its Zenness. Mizoguchi had high expectations of its beauty based on his father’s praises. He was sorely disappointed when he finally saw it and regarded it as ugly. Instead he developed his own mental image of the golden pavilion, an image so perfect that he became obsessed with it.<br /><br />Mizoguchi did not have such a good childhood, with a sickly father, an immoral mother and a stutter, no wonder he was isolated, lonely and alienated. He was a sad, confused dude. So his story was one of conflicts, contrasts and contradictions. He was constantly antagonistic towards the Superior, Father Dosen, although much of it stemmed from his own delusions of persecution. His friends Tsurukawa and Kashiwagi were complete opposites, like an angel and a devil, counseling or misguiding Mizoguchi respectively. Tsurukawa was candid, the voice of reason and Mizoguchi’s conscience. On the other hand, Kashiwagi was devious, manipulative and a bad influence.<br /><br />The story was not solely about the burning of the pavilion. Some of Mishima’s own ruminations were evident within the story. There were a few philosophical discussions about the nature and perception of beauty, mainly through Kashiwagi’s speeches. There was also a misogynistic tone. Each of the women were either presented in a negative light or badly mistreated.<br /><br />So was Mishima successful in presenting Mizoguchi’s story? Yes. I am convinced that Mizoguchi was a nutter. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6621174182077392880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Junta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Junta" href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/162375.The_Temple_Of_The_Golden_Pavilion">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Sociopaths, those visiting the temple in Kyoto</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=japanese">japanese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=read-in-japanese">read-in-japanese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=mishima">mishima</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1301404185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11513985869389608024">There were some interesting philosophical soliloquies and conversations about beauty, identity, life and death, but the story threw me off a little with its pacing. The characters were quite unique, although in a collectively depressing and/or contrived manner. Mizoguchi, a stuttering sociopath, brought the protagonists of Dostoyevsky's <i>Notes from Underground</i> and <i>The Double</i> to my mind.<blockquote>...My appearance may be poor, but in this way my inner world was richer than anyone else's. Isn't it natural for</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11513985869389608024" style="display:none">There were some interesting philosophical soliloquies and conversations about beauty, identity, life and death, but the story threw me off a little with its pacing. The characters were quite unique, although in a collectively depressing and/or contrived manner. Mizoguchi, a stuttering sociopath, brought the protagonists of Dostoyevsky's <i>Notes from Underground</i> and <i>The Double</i> to my mind.<blockquote>...My appearance may be poor, but in this way my inner world was richer than anyone else's. Isn't it natural for a boy with a handicap that cannot very well be dismissed, to think that they are secretly the chosen one? I felt that somewhere in the world, there was a mission I was yet unaware of that awaited me.</blockquote>I hope Mishima's other stories will click with me more (there are nine other stories in his 'Collected Works' [published in 1966, now in my room 50 years later!] I read from) - although I wasn't dazzled, I certainly want to read more by him now. An original style.<br><br>I found the second half a little tedious but I remembered liking the writing in the first few chapters, and there were several memorable quotes. A powerful character who leaves an impression (perhaps more than Mizoguchi) is his clubfooted friend, Kashiwagi - his handicap is much more noticeable than Mizoguchi's, but he's confident, sly and perceptive, knowing what he wants and how he can achieve them. A cool-headed, club-footed asshole.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437305268i/15567887.jpg" width="400" height="533" alt="Kinkakuji" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(Photo taken November 2013)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11513985869389608024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_913538742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/913538742" />
    <a title="Isaac Cooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25791416-isaac-cooper"><img alt="Isaac Cooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393146442p2/25791416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/913538742?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Isaac Cooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Isaac Cooper" href="/user/show/25791416-isaac-cooper">Isaac Cooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25791416-isaac-cooper?shelf=abandoned">abandoned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer913538742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8691030778384831248">I can’t tell you how much, dear reader, I loathe abandoning a book. I don’t do it lightly. It’s not something that I do willy-nilly. It’s not a pleasant experience, abandoning a book. It’s kind of like a break-up. Maybe there was something once in the relationship that really made it work, but you know in your heart you have to break up. Just like I know in my heart I have to stop reading a book, for whatever reason. <br/><br/>Hell, sometimes I abandon books that aren’t terrible, like Blood Meridian or … </span>
  <span id="freeText8691030778384831248" style="display:none">I can’t tell you how much, dear reader, I loathe abandoning a book. I don’t do it lightly. It’s not something that I do willy-nilly. It’s not a pleasant experience, abandoning a book. It’s kind of like a break-up. Maybe there was something once in the relationship that really made it work, but you know in your heart you have to break up. Just like I know in my heart I have to stop reading a book, for whatever reason. <br /><br />Hell, sometimes I abandon books that aren’t terrible, like Blood Meridian or … or this one, Temple of the Golden Pavilion. Just because I abandon doesn’t mean it’s utter tripe. You know how you can tell if a book is utter tripe? If I give it one star. That rating to me is the worst of the worst, the unforgivable and completely irredeemable.<br /><br />So, what I’m saying is that this book wasn’t utterly terrible, is that it?<br /><br />Yeah, that’s right. I enjoy reading foreign books when I get the chance, they’re an interesting change of pace, sure the translation might lose a bit of its original spark, but it’s interesting all the same. This book starts off with an immediately sympathetic character, one who has a stutter and so feels like an outcast at school and wants more than anything for the WWII air raids to flatten Kyoto and Japan along with it. <br /><br />The problem is, and my god did I give it a chance to correct itself (abandoned a little more than half-way through) was that this mother*cker rambles like a … well, like a mother*ucker. It’s just insistent on rambling and often times feels like it’s got not a lot to say in terms of plot or an underlying message. We follow our stuttering-protagonist as a late-adolescent and on to a Buddhist Zen University and his aspirations to one day take over the Golden Temple and so forth, and his so called evil intentions that he wants to release to the world, but it’s just too aimless to work.<br /> <br />This is a truly fragmented book in many ways. It’s hard for me to give it such a low rating when I really do like some of the writing, I think Yukio Mishima was a great writer, he really knew how to write well, but for the life of me I can’t understand why this book is so very aimless. I hear that he originally wrote novels solely for the money, which might give some explanation. The Golden Temple itself really claims the spotlight too much, also. The protagonist is obsessed with it, and finds some connection with his own ugliness and the temple’s beauty, but there has to be more than that. <br /><br />I know in the end, spoilers, I guess, he burns down the temple, but you get to halfway through the book and he’s still admiring it, he’s still going on about the beauty of it and his inherent ugliness and how much he wishes to be evil and so on and so on. It’s interesting to begin with, I was curious for only so long before that curiosity turned in to a simple want for something else to happen. The problem, I think, is that there’s not really a character arc in the book. Okay, so maybe in the end when he burns down the temple? But that’s not a character arc, that’s just a character burning down a temple. <br /><br />My point is there doesn’t feel like any progression from the stuttering beginning of our main character, to what I suppose is meant to be the contradictory act towards the one thing he once admired. Without that arc, there is really little to keep a reader invested. This is not helped by the fact the story is told in the past tense by the main character, as if all of the events have already happened and he is reciting them from a point in the far future. <br /><br />Should you read it? <br /><br />No, I wouldn’t recommend it. There are far better books out there for you, dear reader. Even though I didn’t like it that much, I’d easily recommend Blood Meridian – my last reviewed book – over The Temple of the Golden Pavilion. <br /><br />There’s just too much rambling, too much empty writing to justify the fairly long length of the book. Sure, I said a similar thing about the somewhat repetitiveness of Blood Meridian, but at least in that there is something actively happening, albeit not as quick as I would have wanted.<br /> <br />The Temple of the Golden Pavilion is another book, like Blood Meridian, that really just could have used a big old-fashioned edit. Plain and simple. Remember, it’s always good for a book to be too short than too long. Because with one that’s too long, it’s easy for it to overstay its welcome, to have the reader become fed up with it. One that’s too short, however, leaves you wanting more, keeps you spry. <br /><br />Okay, now I’m rambling and that’s all personal preference anyway. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8691030778384831248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_209851252" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/209851252" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/209851252?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=japanese-fiction">japanese-fiction</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer209851252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2289362119461696914">Only the Japanese can write books like this, I feel. Lyrically beautiful, aesthetically driven, thoroughly homoerotic, unsettlingly fascist at points, violently modernist, violently anti-modernist, violently violent, operatically melodramatic, sexually repressed, profoundly Buddhist, religious, anti-religious, and set in a landscape of burned temples and told by a misanthropic outsider. Lord I want to go there.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5553786821039719179">I saw "Mishima: A Life in Four Chapters" and now I'm re-reading this because I enjoyed it so much. Even bought the soundtrack.   <span id="freeText5553786821039719179" style="display:none">I saw "Mishima: A Life in Four Chapters" and now I'm re-reading this because I enjoyed it so much. Even bought the soundtrack. Would buy the DVD, but it isn't in my region. Annoying.<br /><br />======<br /><br />I only returned to this because I enjoyed "Mishima: A Life in Four Chapters" so much. The film includes a version of this book which does everything necessary in under 15 minutes. I got more from the film than I did reading the book. Someone has edited most, but not all, of the "Temple of the Golden Pavilion" scenes together here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QS25_IF1l4o&amp;feature=related">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QS25_I...</a><br /><br />Mizoguchi narrates the book and there's a lot of what he is thinking. The story is one man and his temple, and I would have liked a few more human relationships. But perhaps priests with interesting human relationships don't burn down temples? <br /><br />The bits of the book I really liked were provided by two female characters, Uiko and a woman at the Tenju Hermitage. Both arrived abruptly and dramatically (Uiko in a sort of public love-suicide, the other serves breast milk to her military beau at the Tenju Hermitage). They then appear as refrains in the rest of the story. I also liked Tsurukawa and his story. In fact, Tsurukawa’s loneliness, unhappiness and confused thoughts about beauty may have been more interesting than Mizoguchi's. <br /><br />"I really was no longer sure whether I was intending to lose my virginity so that I could set fire to the Golden Temple or whether I was planning to burn the Golden Temple in order to lose my virginity."<br /><br />"Perhaps for Uiko death had been merely a temporary incident. The blood that she had left on the gallery of the Kongo Temple had perhaps been something like the powder that remains from a butterfly's wings when one opens the window in the morning and it instantly flies away."<br /></span>

<div id="review_2104509445" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2104509445" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2104509445?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2104509445" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer871529146993189987"><blockquote>
  <i>“I had already recognized that I, who in my middle-school days had deliberately scratched the scabbard of my schoolmate’s sword, was not qualified to enter life through its bright surface. It was Kashiwagi who had first taught me the dark by-way along which I could reach life from the back. …As she drummed Kashiwagi’s misdeeds into my ears and told me of all the cloying sordidness of his behaviour towards her, the single word that I heard resounding in the night air was—‘life.’” </i>
</blockquote> <br/><i>The Temple </i></span>
  <span id="freeText871529146993189987" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“I had already recognized that I, who in my middle-school days had deliberately scratched the scabbard of my schoolmate’s sword, was not qualified to enter life through its bright surface. It was Kashiwagi who had first taught me the dark by-way along which I could reach life from the back. …As she drummed Kashiwagi’s misdeeds into my ears and told me of all the cloying sordidness of his behaviour towards her, the single word that I heard resounding in the night air was—‘life.’” </i>
</blockquote> <br /><i>The Temple of the Golden Pavilion</i> is a quiet, powerful book about a young Buddhist acolyte and his struggle to come to terms with life. It is full of penetrating insight, especially with respect to philosophical matters such as the importance of Beauty, the seductive nature of nihilism, and the disparity between abstract perfection and flawed, complicated reality. It also delved deeply into the psychology of alienation, obsession, and destruction. <br /><br />This book snuck up on me. In that sense, reading it <i>felt</i> similar to what I experienced with Cormac McCarthy’s <i>Suttree</i>. Both books started out slow, but somewhere along the line (I’m not quite sure where), they managed to get their claws into me. And I found that I savored the various scrapes and bruises that I received from those aforementioned claws—I knew that my mind would be left with some exquisite scars in the end. For as my brain was chewing on the book, the book was slowly, relentlessly gnawing on me. Quality literature should strive for no less.</span>
  <a data-text-id="871529146993189987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DkuMvo9nIfuBIgBpyigOH6gBjNstEaIypBQ4zdAAKC80beVsNDnokyYJtAg93QTAfl5rE+OcLFX/an58HUPatQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rbNwaQmVKvR3vN0AvfL4ySSyUy3RFSXAC2xzYBU/tZCXlRm7ssvjnNCXaWFKB/IW8u205R+Yq6dQEjXR2HxHCg==')})
  }

In consequence, he feels himself &quot;disqualified from being human&quot; (a literal translation of the Japanese title).\n\nDonald Keene, who translated this and Dazai\'s first novel, The Setting Sun, has said of the author\'s work: &quot;His world … suggests Chekhov or possibly postwar France, … but there is a Japanese sensibility in the choice and presentation of the material. A Dazai novel is at once immediately intelligible in Western terms and quite unlike any Western book.&quot; His writing is in some ways reminiscent of Rimbaud, while he himself has often been called a Osamu Dazai’s The Setting Sun takes this milieu as its background to tell the story of the decline of a minor aristocratic family.\n\nThe story is told through the eyes of Kazuko, the unmarried daughter of a widowed aristocrat. Her search for self meaning in a society devoid of use for her forms the crux of Dazai’s novel. It is a sad story, and structurally is a novel very much within the confines of the Japanese take on the novel in a way reminiscent of authors such as Nobel Prize winner Yasunari Kawabata – the social interactions are peripheral and understated, nuances must be drawn, and for readers more used to Western novelistic forms this comes across as being rather wishy-washy.\n\nKazuko’s mother falls ill, and due to their financial circumstances they are forced to take a cottage in the countryside. Her brother, who became addicted to opium during the war is missing. When he returns, Kazuko attempts to form a liaison with the novelist Uehara. The relationships which have previously defined his life - with his son, his wife, and his attractive daughter-in-law - are dissolving, and Shingo is caught between love and destruction. Lyrical and precise, The Sound of the Mountain explores in immaculately crafted prose the changing roles of love and the truth we face in ageing.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13148625638160737525\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/35258.Yukio_Mishima">About Yukio Mishima</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/35258.Yukio_Mishima"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1502325778p3/35258.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/35258.Yukio_Mishima">Yukio Mishima
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,408
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5816553716858914722">Yukio Mishima (三島 由紀夫) was born in Tokyo in 1925. He graduated from Tokyo Imperial University’s School of Jurisprudence in 1947. His first published book, <i>The Forest in Full Bloom</i>, appeared in 1944 and he established himself as a major author with <i>Confessions of a Mask</i> (1949). From then until his death he continued to publish novels, short stories, and plays each year. His crowning achievement, th</span>
  <span id="freeText5816553716858914722" style="display:none">Yukio Mishima (三島 由紀夫) was born in Tokyo in 1925. He graduated from Tokyo Imperial University’s School of Jurisprudence in 1947. His first published book, <i>The Forest in Full Bloom</i>, appeared in 1944 and he established himself as a major author with <i>Confessions of a Mask</i> (1949). From then until his death he continued to publish novels, short stories, and plays each year. His crowning achievement, the Sea of Fertility tetralogy—which contains the novels <i>Spring Snow</i> (1969), <i>Runaway Horses</i> (1969), <i>The Temple of Dawn</i> (1970), and <i>The Decay of the Angel</i> (1971)—is considered one of the definitive works of twentieth-century Japanese fiction. In 1970, at the age of forty-five and the day after completing the last novel in the Fertility series, Mishima committed seppuku (ritual suicide)—a spectacular death that attracted worldwide attention.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5816553716858914722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
